  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1351233667" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <span id="freeText5286397460841396925" style="display:none">ساق البامبو = The Bamboo Stalk, سعود السنعوسی = Saud Alsanousi<br />Josephine comes to Kuwait from the Philippines to work as a maid, where she meets Rashid, a spoiled only son. Josephine, with all the wide-eyed naivety of youth, believes she has found true love. But when she becomes pregnant, and with the rumble of war growing ever louder, Rashid abandons her and sends her back home with her baby son, Jose. Brought up struggling with his dual identity, Jose clings to the hope of returning to his father's country when he is 18. But will Kuwait be any more welcoming to him? Daring and bold, 'The Bamboo Stalk' takes an unflinching look at the phenomenon of foreign workers in Arab countries and confronts the universal problems of identity, race and religion.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: ماه آگوست سال 2015 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: ساقه بامبو؛ اثر: سعود السنعوسی؛ مترجم: مریم اکبری؛ عظیم طهماسبی؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، نیلوفر، 1393، در 438 ص، شابک 9789644486173؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان عرب - سده 20 م<br /><br />نخستین رمان عربی نبود که خوانده ام، با این وجود نمیتوانستم صبوری کنم، تا آسوده دراز بکشم، و با یک لیوان آب خنک نیز، هیجان خود را فرو بنشانم، و سپس داستان را بخوانم. میدانستم که در زبان عرب، به «بامبو»، خیزران میگویند، بیشتر میخواستم بدانم چرا نام کتاب: «ساق البامبو» شده، خواندم و زمین نگذاشتم. ساقه بامبو، قهرمان جوانی دارد، که از پدری کویتی، و مادری فیلیپینی (مستخدمه)، زاده شده، و در کشور فیلیپین بالیده، و به دنبال هویت خویش میگردد، اثری جسورانه است. به پدیده ی کارگری مهاجران، در کشورهای حوزه ی خلیج فارس، و به بررسی هویت آنان در این کشورها میپردازد ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_1192482263" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1192482263" />
    <a title="Susan Abulhawa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24108689-susan-abulhawa"><img alt="Susan Abulhawa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596991915p2/24108689.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1192482263?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan Abulhawa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan Abulhawa" href="/user/show/24108689-susan-abulhawa">Susan Abulhawa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789927101779" href="/book/show/23168853-the-bamboo-stalk">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1192482263" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9075399874153917790">The following review was published in the Philadelphia Inquirer on September 27, 2015:<br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.philly.com/philly/entertainment/sunday/20150927__Bamboo_Stalk__by_Saud_Alsanousi__Penetrating_look_at_Arab_world_s_issues_of_race_and_class.html'>http://www.philly.com/philly/entertai...</a><br/><br/>The Bamboo Stalk by Saud Alsanousi is now in English, a celebrated novel in the Arabic-speaking world. Winner of the International Prize for Arabic Fiction, it reads like a memoir of the protagonist, José Mendoza, whose name is also Isa Al-Tarouf - one name for each aspect of his identity.<br/>José/Isa is born to a Filipino domestic servant and the only son of her employer, Gh</span>
  <span id="freeText9075399874153917790" style="display:none">The following review was published in the Philadelphia Inquirer on September 27, 2015:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.philly.com/philly/entertainment/sunday/20150927__Bamboo_Stalk__by_Saud_Alsanousi__Penetrating_look_at_Arab_world_s_issues_of_race_and_class.html">http://www.philly.com/philly/entertai...</a><br /><br />The Bamboo Stalk by Saud Alsanousi is now in English, a celebrated novel in the Arabic-speaking world. Winner of the International Prize for Arabic Fiction, it reads like a memoir of the protagonist, José Mendoza, whose name is also Isa Al-Tarouf - one name for each aspect of his identity.<br />José/Isa is born to a Filipino domestic servant and the only son of her employer, Ghanima, a dour Kuwaiti matriarch of a prominent Kuwaiti family. Rejected by his grandmother, the mother and son are sent back to the Philippines.<br /><br />From the protagonist's beginnings in the Philippines to his return to Kuwait, the atmosphere, aesthetics, and colors of the character's internal and external worlds seem to mirror the landscape in each region. One is lush and green, bustling with life and family. The other is severe and emotionally barren, a desert littered with trip wires of racism, classism, and the oppressive tribal ethos of Kuwait's elite.<br /><br />Though characters in the first half are more compelling, it tends to seem like a setup to the inevitable reckoning coming in Kuwait. The second half is more interesting, precisely because of its emotional complexity.<br /><br />By the time José returns to Kuwait, Iraqi forces have killed his father. He travels with the help of Ghassan, his late father's friend who is a Bidoon, a socially outcast minority without citizenship or national rights.<br /><br />José/Isa's attempts to win his Kuwaiti family's favor are ultimately futile, even though he is their only possible male heir. The family is cruel in their rejection. Interestingly, the social complications are explained in depth, as are various social and religious mores, even though the original version was for Arabic-speaking readers who already understood these underpinnings. Alsanousi clearly intends to confront and challenge his own society on racism toward imported laborers and the treatment of them that has been likened to modern-day slavery. But it seems he writes with non-Arab readers also in mind.<br /><br />My only real criticism is that José/Isa is so obsequious. His desire to be included in anything Kuwaiti is insufferable. When he makes Kuwaiti friends, he can hardly contain his happiness and incredulity: "My friends. Kuwaitis. Kuwaitis. Kuwaitis." Such deference strips him of agency, tends to perpetuate stereotypes, and serves the inflated Kuwaiti sense of self.<br /><br />Still, I regard Saud Alsanousi as an important emerging literary voice in the Arabian Gulf region. This novel has been difficult to keep on the shelves in Arabic bookstores, suggesting he speaks to a large readership that wants open discussions about social and political issues. For literary merit and social impact, he deserves his accolades.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9075399874153917790" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2195506599" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Faroukh Naseem" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47239298-faroukh-naseem"><img alt="Faroukh Naseem" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588328903p2/47239298.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2195506599?book_show_action=true">Nov 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Faroukh Naseem" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Faroukh Naseem" href="/user/show/47239298-faroukh-naseem">Faroukh Naseem</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789927101779" href="/book/show/23168853-the-bamboo-stalk">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2195506599" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6346902224963696848">Firstly let me just say: If you are looking for Arab fiction, you don’t need my review to convince you to pick this up, just get hold of a copy and start reading.<br/>.<br/>#theguywiththebookreview presents The Bamboo Stalk<br/>.<br/>Saud Alsanousi takes us through the struggles of Isa(in Kuwait)/Jesus(in Philippines), a Kuwaiti-Filipino boy torn apart by identity crisis. Back in Philippines he’s known as the Kuwaiti and in Kuwait he isn’t accepted as one since he looks more Pinoy than Kuwaiti.<br/>.<br/>A conflict between c</span>
  <span id="freeText6346902224963696848" style="display:none">Firstly let me just say: If you are looking for Arab fiction, you don’t need my review to convince you to pick this up, just get hold of a copy and start reading.<br />.<br />#theguywiththebookreview presents The Bamboo Stalk<br />.<br />Saud Alsanousi takes us through the struggles of Isa(in Kuwait)/Jesus(in Philippines), a Kuwaiti-Filipino boy torn apart by identity crisis. Back in Philippines he’s known as the Kuwaiti and in Kuwait he isn’t accepted as one since he looks more Pinoy than Kuwaiti.<br />.<br />A conflict between culture and what is right ensues and Isa is right in the middle of it. A flurry of intriguing characters appear in just the right proportion to keep the book well measured and balanced beautifully to keep the focus on Isa.<br />.<br />Here’s what I liked:<br />▪Very well thought out and researched book.<br />▪It almost seems like the events actually happened, makes it feel like it’s an autobiography.<br />▪Absolutely amazing characters, so different from what most popular/western books have.<br />▪This book has perhaps the best final chapter I’ve read of any book, the way the last chapter takes us back to the first and winds the book tight is beautiful!<br />.<br />Here’s what I didn’t like:<br />▫This book, might read as cultural commentary, might give it a generalized feel about both countries (Kuwait and Phillipines) especially to readers who haven’t read more from these areas. Somewhat like how The Kite Runner and A Thousand Splendid Suns almost seem like text book manuals about Afghanistan.<br />.<br />Having said that, the portrayal of the Kuwaiti lifestyle and people is on point and this would make an intriguing read for people who arent from or haven’t been to the Middle East.</span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10248384359492894537">So this is my first required university read and I am so glad I enjoyed it. The writing was smooth and intricate which had the story easily flowing. The words were simple enough to understand yet they held such deep meanings. This story broke my heart and made me feel various feelings from good to bad. All in all it was a good book with much diversity and honesty from them lives of those who are considered ‘lesser’ in our society. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1742642336" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1742642336" />
    <a title="DubaiReader" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2368681-dubaireader"><img alt="DubaiReader" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1262556200p2/2368681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1742642336?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="DubaiReader" class="user" itemprop="url" name="DubaiReader" href="/user/show/2368681-dubaireader">DubaiReader</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/25507552-the-bamboo-stalk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2368681-dubaireader?shelf=e-book">e-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2368681-dubaireader?shelf=lit-fest-author">lit-fest-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2368681-dubaireader?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1742642336" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3862619801015055014">An excellent insight into life in Kuwait and The Philippines.<br/><br/>I've just finished this and I was pretty impressed by the way the author managed to show the full extent of Jose's position as a Philippino/Kuwaiti, both in the Philippines and in Kuwait. I was also surprised to find that this is in fact a translation from Arabic, so full marks to Jonathan Wright, the translator. <br/><br/>Jose's mother, Josephine, was a Philippina maid in the Al-Tarouf household in Kuwait, when she fell in love with Rashid, the</span>
  <span id="freeText3862619801015055014" style="display:none">An excellent insight into life in Kuwait and The Philippines.<br /><br />I've just finished this and I was pretty impressed by the way the author managed to show the full extent of Jose's position as a Philippino/Kuwaiti, both in the Philippines and in Kuwait. I was also surprised to find that this is in fact a translation from Arabic, so full marks to Jonathan Wright, the translator. <br /><br />Jose's mother, Josephine, was a Philippina maid in the Al-Tarouf household in Kuwait, when she fell in love with Rashid, the wayward, only son. Rashid loved his baby son but his mother was horrified and turned him out of the house. Not surprisingly, Josephine lost her job and she and her son were deported back home. <br />There is no news from Rashid for many years and Josephine's searches come back cold. But although Jose is raised in the Philippines, he always carries his father's promise that he will eventually return to be a son of Kuwait.<br /><br />The book description reveals that he does manage to return to Kuwait, as a young man, so I'm not giving away any spoilers there. What he finds though, is probably the book's strongest part - it shows how the people are not primarily Kuwaiti, nor from a particular sect or class, their strongest allegiance is to the family name and it is pride in this that rules all their actions. Nothing must bring shame on the family, and a half Philippino son is not something to be proud of.<br /><br />I can see why The Bamboo Stalk was winner of the 2013 International Prize for Arab Fiction - chosen both for its literary qualities and for “its social and humanitarian content.” <br />It is an excellent insight into the issues behind the phenomenon of expat workers into wealthy GCC countries.<br />Definitely recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3862619801015055014" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Having lived in the Middle East for almost a decade now I found this story particularly interesting and can totally understand the situations that Isa found himself in and the controversy he faced. A really interesting read. </span>

<div id="review_3167090316" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3167090316" />
    <a title="Lauren " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1291915-lauren"><img alt="Lauren " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604235645p2/1291915.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3167090316?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren " href="/user/show/1291915-lauren">Lauren </a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/35683588-the-bamboo-stalk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1291915-lauren?shelf=rtw21">rtw21</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1291915-lauren?shelf=arab-arabic-lit">arab-arabic-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1291915-lauren?shelf=translated-works">translated-works</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3167090316" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12021527707899815795"><i>"I was a bamboo plant... You cut off a piece of the stalk and plant it without roots in any piece of ground. Before long the stalk sprouts new roots and starts to grow again in the new ground, with no past, no memory."</i><br/><br/>THE BAMBOO STALK by Saud Alsanousi, tr. from the Arabic (Kuwait) by Jonathan Wright, 2012 Arabic / 2015 English.<br/><br/>This coming-of-age novel follows young José/Isa, the child of Rashid, a Kuwaiti, and Josephine, a Filipina. José is raised in The Philippines by his aunt, grandfather, an</span>
  <span id="freeText12021527707899815795" style="display:none"><i>"I was a bamboo plant... You cut off a piece of the stalk and plant it without roots in any piece of ground. Before long the stalk sprouts new roots and starts to grow again in the new ground, with no past, no memory."</i><br /><br />THE BAMBOO STALK by Saud Alsanousi, tr. from the Arabic (Kuwait) by Jonathan Wright, 2012 Arabic / 2015 English.<br /><br />This coming-of-age novel follows young José/Isa, the child of Rashid, a Kuwaiti, and Josephine, a Filipina. José is raised in The Philippines by his aunt, grandfather, and cousin. He migrates back to Kuwait as a young man to meet the other side of his family, embracing his Arabic name, Isa.<br /><br />We see both The Philippines and Kuwait through José/Isa's eyes - the beauties, but also the flaws - hardships, societal boundaries. Unfortunately his childhood and youth are a struggle as he never "fits" in to either world. Isa's narration is very engaging, and the book flies by as we experience life with this young man, his spiritual awakenings, his search for connection, and inevitably the strength to move on through adversity. <br /><br />Recommended for learning more about foreign workers in the Gulf, about Kuwait's history both before and after Iraqi occupation, and established social and cultural systems.<br /><br />Hoping to find some more English translations from the Gulf Nations, and Arabian peninsula... A few titles out there, but still a very small number compared to other regions!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12021527707899815795" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17997185062764101692">The first half of the book meanders along telling the back story of José Mendoza born to a Filipino mother who worked as a maid in the house of his Kuwaiti father. Thrown out by the family, Jose bides his time in Manilla waiting for the opportunity to return to Kuwait, be reunited with his father's family and live in the land of plenty. The inevitable occurs and Jose, now Isa Al-Tarouf, returns to a frosty welcome as the bastard child of a now dead father. <br/>The book then focuses on the Kuwaiti's </span>
  <span id="freeText17997185062764101692" style="display:none">The first half of the book meanders along telling the back story of José Mendoza born to a Filipino mother who worked as a maid in the house of his Kuwaiti father. Thrown out by the family, Jose bides his time in Manilla waiting for the opportunity to return to Kuwait, be reunited with his father's family and live in the land of plenty. The inevitable occurs and Jose, now Isa Al-Tarouf, returns to a frosty welcome as the bastard child of a now dead father. <br />The book then focuses on the Kuwaiti's class system and of the fear of having their family tainted by scandal. It also explores the problem of Isa/Jose not being able to fit in within either of his world's due to his appearance. <br />Isa is so enamoured by Kuwait it becomes tedious. But as this is a rare book in English about life in modern Kuwait this book is worth reading.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3521351241" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3510976958254190979">This sad but engaging novel is about mixed cultures focusing on the treatment of minorities in Kuwaiti. <br/>Jose aka Isa is the son of an upper class Kuwaiti who secretly married his Filipina maid. <br/>His family forces his father to divorce his mother and send her along with baby Jose back to the Philippines. For a while the father sends money but then it stops. Jose's mother raises him with the hope that one day he will return to Kuwait. <br/>Tne first part of the novel takes places in the Philippines. I w</span>
  <span id="freeText3510976958254190979" style="display:none">This sad but engaging novel is about mixed cultures focusing on the treatment of minorities in Kuwaiti. <br />Jose aka Isa is the son of an upper class Kuwaiti who secretly married his Filipina maid. <br />His family forces his father to divorce his mother and send her along with baby Jose back to the Philippines. For a while the father sends money but then it stops. Jose's mother raises him with the hope that one day he will return to Kuwait. <br />Tne first part of the novel takes places in the Philippines. I was impressed how much the author seemed to know about the country. <br />The second half of the novel takes place in Kuwait. It's revealed that Jose's father died in captivity after the Gulf war and his dying wish was to bring Jose back to Kuwait. The father's family does not accept him and although they grudgingly take him in, he has to live in the annex and has to keep secret that he is part if the family.<br />The author is clearly very critical of Kuwait society and I applaud him for this. <br />Although I am fairly knowledgeable about the Gulf states, as well as the treatment of foreign workers, I learned a lot. I highly recommend this book although parts are sad and infuriating!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3510976958254190979" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1333862331" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1333862331" />
    <a title="Tripfiction" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24495694-tripfiction"><img alt="Tripfiction" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514316307p2/24495694.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1333862331?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tripfiction" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tripfiction" href="/user/show/24495694-tripfiction">Tripfiction</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789927101779" href="/book/show/23168853-the-bamboo-stalk">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1333862331" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2671465466184397363">Novel set in Kuwait and The Philippines (“..epic in its breadth and story..”)<br/><br/>This is an incredible story of migration and rootlessness and what it means “to belong”. It is epic in its breadth and story, and explores how two unequal cultures are inextricably linked through economic need. And still, it is so much more.<br/><br/>Josephine, originally from the Philippines, is working in Kuwait for the al-Tarouf family. The father is long dead, the matriarch, stuck in traditional values, lives with her four ch</span>
  <span id="freeText2671465466184397363" style="display:none">Novel set in Kuwait and The Philippines (“..epic in its breadth and story..”)<br /><br />This is an incredible story of migration and rootlessness and what it means “to belong”. It is epic in its breadth and story, and explores how two unequal cultures are inextricably linked through economic need. And still, it is so much more.<br /><br />Josephine, originally from the Philippines, is working in Kuwait for the al-Tarouf family. The father is long dead, the matriarch, stuck in traditional values, lives with her four children, son Rashid and 3 daughters. Rashid is clearly struggling with the rigid strictures of home and culture and takes the Filipana maid as his wife, unacceptable to family and society. When they go on to have a child, José (Isa), whose story this is, the pressure becomes too much and mother and child are summarily despatched back to The Philippines.<br /><br />José spends what seems to be his entire childhood just waiting for his Father to summon him back to Kuwait. He has a limbo existence, uprooted and deposited, trying to straddle the duality of culture. Every time he has the merest chance of an encounter with Kuwaitis, he takes it. On Boracay – an island I visited 30 years ago, when the landing strip at Caticlan had to be cleared of chickens – he takes a menial job, and spends a pivotal evening with a group of young men from Kuwait. But time and again, those around him struggle to accept him as a member of either nationality, his looks, learning and life experience are all inhibitors to integration and acceptance.<br /><br />Alsanousi has a real ability to get into thinking of his chosen countries, the Philippines just comes alive, the almost insignificant interactions between José’s family members, their squabbles and traumas express more than the sum of the words. In Kuwait he captures the oppressive nature of society, the small country reflected in mores and manners, and how prejudice threads its way right through the layers of class, but how fragile it can all feel.<br /><br />Much credit goes to the translator Jonathan Wright<br /><br />One of the best books I have read in recent time, I can only say go out and buy it!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2671465466184397363" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer13354774660001599671"><b>UPDATE, 6 FEB 2016</b><br/><br/>An English version is now available. <br/><br/><br/><b>ORIGINAL REVIEW</b><br/><br/>The Arabic Booker Prize Winner of 2013 is in short the personal diaspora of lost identity, or to be more accurate DUAL IDENTITY. The protagonist here is always stamped by his OTHER origins. Wherever he goes, he is told that he belongs to the other side. Both sides think of the other as a disgrace, although both sides hold more similarities between them than differences. But it became common behavior that when it comes to diffe</span>
  <span id="freeText13354774660001599671" style="display:none"><b>UPDATE, 6 FEB 2016</b><br /><br />An English version is now available. <br /><br /><br /><b>ORIGINAL REVIEW</b><br /><br />The Arabic Booker Prize Winner of 2013 is in short the personal diaspora of lost identity, or to be more accurate DUAL IDENTITY. The protagonist here is always stamped by his OTHER origins. Wherever he goes, he is told that he belongs to the other side. Both sides think of the other as a disgrace, although both sides hold more similarities between them than differences. But it became common behavior that when it comes to different cultures we tend to emphasize our differences to the extent of escalating conflicts as a way of aiming at superiority, forgetting that we all are humans in the end and each one of us has a good side and a bad side.<br /><br />What I liked most about this book is the historical side of the Philippines and how every country is similar to one another in the way it cherishes its heroes. The political background of the characters and their patriotic past contributed to the unity of the plot immensely. Also, the discrepancies in the Kuwaiti families social system leads some of its members to personal estrangement, even if they don't admit it.</span>

<div id="review_722832311" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/722832311" />
    <a title="Noha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2738919-noha"><img alt="Noha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425935817p2/2738919.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/722832311?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Noha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Noha" href="/user/show/2738919-noha">Noha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2738919-noha?shelf=%D8%A8%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%B9%D8%B1%D8%A8%D9%8A">بالعربي</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer722832311" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8427286461216234743">So, I may be a little bit biased for my 5-star rating. I was so emotionally invested in reading, I can't think clearly. It opened wounds I wanted to hide their invisible scars. The identity crisis Josè Mendosa has may sound weird to some people, they would argue that the story plot is unrealistic, a khaleeji drama or simply an exaggeration from the author's imagination but it described how lost I feel. The protagonist is a product of transnational marriage which one parent is a GCC citizen. For </span>
  <span id="freeText8427286461216234743" style="display:none">So, I may be a little bit biased for my 5-star rating. I was so emotionally invested in reading, I can't think clearly. It opened wounds I wanted to hide their invisible scars. The identity crisis Josè Mendosa has may sound weird to some people, they would argue that the story plot is unrealistic, a khaleeji drama or simply an exaggeration from the author's imagination but it described how lost I feel. The protagonist is a product of transnational marriage which one parent is a GCC citizen. For those of you who is not involved in GCC politics, transnational kids are considered second-class citizens, they are neither belong to their mothers' nor their fathers' countries. If that parent happened to be the father, naturally the kid would have his father's nationality (Like Josè) but it's NOT the case if it was the mother. Some Arab countries believe citizenship only granted through the father even if the kid was born outside that country, again this is not the case with if it was the mother; her kids will be granted their father citizenship even if they were born in her country! So basically, jus solis and jus sanguinis is not applicable there!! Please don't let me started how shattering self-loathing this delimma can cause to a person to be born like this, like Josè.. Like me!!<br /><br />His words and emotions matched my own.. I always felt sorry for myself, but reading this made me realize I may be in better situation than somebody in the world. I may have identity crisis but it never occurred to me how lucky I am for at least I know my place in my world.. I am much proud Muslim as devastating Josè's spiritual journey was.. I knew both of my parents, as much I hated them for forcing me in this ludicrous condition I was showered by love of both of them, together.. We were family of our own misery.. The contrast of my situation with his gave the story my uttermost respect.. I understood what a Bamboo meant.. I am one, and I'm sure any human being would at least sympathize.. The writing style was outstanding compared to my current Arabic reads (the plot, the timeline, margins and the translator move.. Very impressive if you asked me)<br />and both the author and editor deserve my respect for lack of any grammar or spelling mistakes *Standing ovation granted*. <br /><br />Whether you are another Josè or me or not, read it.. It may give you another perspective of how some misfortunate people live.. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8427286461216234743" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText3133259513316063775" style="display:none">I read this book in its original language, Arabic, but I'm reviewing it in English because it's been translated and I want all my English-speaking friends here to read it too.<br /><br />This book is about José, the son of a maid from the Philippines and her employer's son, a rich Kuwaiti man. The book explores the story of a man lost between two homelands, the one he rejects and the other that rejects him. It highlights the elitism, racism and the absurdity of the social hierarchy of Kuwait, and of Arabs in general. It's also a great way to get to know both the Philippines and Kuwait. The book is beautifully written, and it's also presented in an original way.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9615998460013358283">The novel speaks so much about Kuwait and the struggles one can face in Kuwait. It's interesting how it discusses the mysterious power "family name" holds in Kuwait. Also, human rights issues and the "Bidoon" are important themes. The setting is very realistic; the language is simple, but it is rich with paradoxes and philosophical statements. I especially like how the novel is creatively formed.</span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7197848884404045118">This novel is a fictional memoir of the child of a Kuwaiti writer and his family's Filipina maid. After a childhood of yearning to meet the Kuwaiti side of his family, `Isa/Jose finds himself shunned, a victim of misplaced shame. As he navigates his desire for acceptance, he learns that his family is also a victim- a victim of helplessness, albeit a conceded one, towards its society's sometimes ugly norms.<br/><br/>The story lends itself to the memoir form, but such a form proposes the challenge of writin</span>
  <span id="freeText7197848884404045118" style="display:none">This novel is a fictional memoir of the child of a Kuwaiti writer and his family's Filipina maid. After a childhood of yearning to meet the Kuwaiti side of his family, `Isa/Jose finds himself shunned, a victim of misplaced shame. As he navigates his desire for acceptance, he learns that his family is also a victim- a victim of helplessness, albeit a conceded one, towards its society's sometimes ugly norms.<br /><br />The story lends itself to the memoir form, but such a form proposes the challenge of writing fictional introspection. The author often veered into overexplanation, which is always a little insulting to the reader (me). Otherwise, it was a touching and enjoyable read. It taught me a lot about the Philippines and stirred in me a welcome nostalgia for Kuwait, which will always have a special place in my heart.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1221377215" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer18009208889732230802">I read the first 78 pages of this book, which has proven popular in the Arab world. It's about the position of foreign workers and outsiders in Kuwait, though the early sections are set in the Philippines, and it's constructed of short chapters. Unfortunately, I found it unengaging. The narrator describes his family's lives and his childhood and there's not yet a plot to be seen on the horizon. The characters are flat; I read an interview with the author about how we're supposed to love the narr</span>
  <span id="freeText18009208889732230802" style="display:none">I read the first 78 pages of this book, which has proven popular in the Arab world. It's about the position of foreign workers and outsiders in Kuwait, though the early sections are set in the Philippines, and it's constructed of short chapters. Unfortunately, I found it unengaging. The narrator describes his family's lives and his childhood and there's not yet a plot to be seen on the horizon. The characters are flat; I read an interview with the author about how we're supposed to love the narrator, and this was meant to make the criticism of Kuwaiti society easier for Kuwaiti readers to bear. It looks like that has worked for its intended audience, which is excellent, but I never felt anything for the narrator, nor did I find him interesting. Clearly, this is not for me.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2127514743" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2127514743" />
    <a title="Chaimae Elajjani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/69476715-chaimae-elajjani"><img alt="Chaimae Elajjani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500121452p2/69476715.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2127514743?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chaimae Elajjani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chaimae Elajjani" href="/user/show/69476715-chaimae-elajjani">Chaimae Elajjani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789927101779" href="/book/show/23168853-the-bamboo-stalk">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2127514743&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2127514743" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4493551066028925310"><b>" Sometimes I think of those minutes Rashid and Josephine spent on the boat when they become my father and mother. It's madness that their moments of pleasure should make my life such a misery.'' _José Mendoza.</b><br/> The novel’s narrator is José Mendoza, who tells his story between Kuwait and the Philippines, between his father’s homeland and his mother’s. He starts from the time “before his birth,” when his mother Josephine is forced to leave behind her studies and family to go work in Kuwait as a ho</span>
  <span id="freeText4493551066028925310" style="display:none"><b>" Sometimes I think of those minutes Rashid and Josephine spent on the boat when they become my father and mother. It's madness that their moments of pleasure should make my life such a misery.'' _José Mendoza.</b><br /> The novel’s narrator is José Mendoza, who tells his story between Kuwait and the Philippines, between his father’s homeland and his mother’s. He starts from the time “before his birth,” when his mother Josephine is forced to leave behind her studies and family to go work in Kuwait as a household servant. Here, she meets Rashid al-Taruf, a kind and cultivated man. They fall in love and get married in secret and they give birth to José-- Issa. Unfortunatley, 2 years after Issa's birth, Josephine will be forced to leave Kuwait and go back to the Philippines because Rashid's mother, hated Josephine and her son and saw them as bad luck bringers. Though José lived in the Phiilippines for years, his mother didn't stop telling him about his father's homeland and he shall go back there when the right time comes.<br /> At age eighteen, after Rashid's death, José finally manages go to Kuwait. With his Kuwaiti passport, Issa is not really an immigrant, but he still suffers from discrimination and isolation. His features are Asian and he cannot speak Arabic. He cannot communicate easily with his relatives from his father’s side, which sets him apart from an environment that has never really welcomed him.Not only does he have to face the racist attitudes of Kuwaiti society, but he has also to struggle against the hostility of his grandmother and aunts, who are worried that his presence could irreparably damage their family’s reputation. All his childhood dreams shatter and crumble because his father's homeland is not really what he expected; he expected it to be an Eldorado and Paradiso but it turns out to be an Inferno. The description of Issa/José’s experience in Kuwait gives the author the chance to give an account of the miserable conditions of migrant workers in Gulf countries. It also spots light on sacrifices that are done by immigrants for the sake of not being deported to their home countries; the example of the Filipina woman that he met at the police station, who sells her body to policemen for the exchange of setting her free.<br />''<b> If only my parents could have given me a one single, clear identity, instead of making me grope my way alone through life in search of one.</b>"<br /> The protagonist is a true example of an identity crisis. He has a hybrid identity and he puts into question his own existence. He isn't even sure about which religion to embrace. Brought up a Christian, finds out about Buddhism, and lately introduced to Islam in Kuwait. But then he finds himself lost, '' which one is the true Islam ?'-- the Sunni path, the Shi'a one, the Islam of Abbu Sayaf group, or the Sufi path of Lapu-lapu ?. Issa, or José as he likes to be called, is filled with confusion, fear and doubt.<br /> '' <b>I was more like a bamboo plant who doesn't belong anywhere in particular.</b>' '<br /> The title of the novel ''The Bamboo Stalk'' sums up everything, because José is like a Bamboo stalk. Bamboo plants don't belong anywhere in particular, you can cut off a piece of the stalk and plant it without roots in any peace of land and soon it grows up with no past and no memory. Even the plant itself has different names-- just like José/Issa, it is called Kawayan in the Philippines, Khaizuran in Kuwait, and Bamboo in many places.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4493551066028925310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_1467423922" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1467423922" />
    <a title="Kath" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6090460-kath"><img alt="Kath" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417509169p2/6090460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1467423922?book_show_action=true">Dec 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kath" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kath" href="/user/show/6090460-kath">Kath</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789927101779" href="/book/show/23168853-the-bamboo-stalk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6090460-kath?shelf=ramadan-reading-list">ramadan-reading-list</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1467423922" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15759160849524465650">I have been waiting for this book to come out in English since 2012 when it won the International Prize for Arabic Fiction. Finally, finally, I received the English translation, made possible because of the prize.<br/><br/>Having lived in Kuwait for 8 years, I was intensely curious to see how a Kuwaiti man would tell the story of a boy whose father was Kuwaiti and whose mother, although the two were legally married, was still the result of the father's union with the family's Filipina maid.<br/><br/>As it turns out</span>
  <span id="freeText15759160849524465650" style="display:none">I have been waiting for this book to come out in English since 2012 when it won the International Prize for Arabic Fiction. Finally, finally, I received the English translation, made possible because of the prize.<br /><br />Having lived in Kuwait for 8 years, I was intensely curious to see how a Kuwaiti man would tell the story of a boy whose father was Kuwaiti and whose mother, although the two were legally married, was still the result of the father's union with the family's Filipina maid.<br /><br />As it turns out, Al Sanousi's tale is realistic, believable, and as heart-breaking as I thought it would be. Afraid of the shame the half-blood child will bring to the family name, the father is forced by his domineering mother to send the mother and son packing back to the islands, shortly before he goes missing in the 1990 invasion.<br /><br />Jose grows up, fatherless in his motherland, never quite feeling complete, always dreaming of the day his father will make good on his promise to raise him as his legitimate Kuwaiti heir.<br /><br />When that time arrives, Jose becomes Isa Al Tarouf, and is literally caught in the net of the Tarouf family reputation. What ensues is a Juvenalian satire of the state of class and race relations in Kuwait. Al Sanousi creates a microcosm of the microcosm that is Kuwait--with the bad, as well as so much that is good.<br /><br />It is a first novel, so perhaps some of its elements are a bit contrived. For example, the attempt to connect the colonization of the Philippines to the lack of father figures, is a bit of a stretch, but it does ultimately work for the "islands" part of the story as it resolves. The main motif, the titular "bamboo stalk" is made a bit too obvious in places. <br /><br />However, the historical backdrop and discussion of Bidoon and Kuwaiti politics from 2006-2008 was dead-on accurate, as I was teaching during those years and remember those elections quite well. <br /><br />Was I surprised at the outcome of the novel? No. No, I wasn't.<br /><br />Was I disappointed? Yes, I was.<br /><br />Oh, Kuwait. You are a small world, indeed, as the book so clearly reminds us. I wish the novel would become required reading for all students in Kuwait; however, I fear the opposite may be more likely to happen. Tongues wag. Reputation is more important than money.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15759160849524465650" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FVjswwgGgsXxChmY8naDq7uXl4oz1Q86UMyO0i0Us90vfoURs1hLrVYhrfkFg4l0bchwQv1YgV0Lsshj4FdBRw==')})
    }
  }

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

id=\"freeText7648373018374114639\" style=\"display:none\">&quot;أجلس اليوم إلى جوارك، أندب أحلامي الحمقى.. غارقة في حبي لك ولا قدرة لي على انتشال بقايا أحلامي من بين حطامك..&quot;، &quot;أحببتك أكثر بتفاصيل مشاعرها تجاه الرجل الذي تحب، والأهم أنها تحاول التعبير عن حالتها الذهنية في تناقضها الحتمي مع حالة الحب الذي تكنّ. فالشرخ الذي يولّد عذابات جمة لا دواء لها، لا بد قائم بين عقلها وقوة مشاعرها، وعطائها الذي لا حدود له للمحبوب: &quot;كنت على استعداد لأن أصغر فتكبر..لأن أفشل لتنجح، لأن أخبو لتلمع..&quot;، ونوعية حبها الذي لا يقدره بل يتجاهله أو يقوم باستغلاله لصالح أهداف أقل أهمية منه بكثير، وأقل قيمة. &quot;ما زلت لا أدرك، لا أدرك كيف يتلاعب رجل بامرأة تحبه من دون أن يخاف للحظة مما يفعله نحوها!&quot;\n\nفي كل مرة و&quot;بعد كل خيبة أمل.. بعد كل محنة وكل نزوة.. كنت أحاول لملمة أجزائي لنفتح مجدداً صفحة بيضاء أخرى..&quot;، لكن &quot;البدايات الجديدة ما هي إلا كذبة&quot;.\n\nتستعيد الكاتبة ذكرياتها معه منذ البداية في مقهى خارج الوطن الذي غادرته للدراسة: &quot;الوطن الذي لو لم أغادره لما حدث كل هذا.. أتكون أنت عقابي على مغادرة وطن أحبني!..&quot;، وفكرة العقاب والثواب التي يسخر منها عزيز، تؤمن هي بها: &quot;مؤمنة أنا بيوم الحساب أكثر من أي شيء،&quot; لأنها تأمل عبرها أن ينال عزيز عقابه على ما اقترفه بحقها.\n\nقالت لهما العجوز الهندية غريبة الأطوار، دون سابق معرفة: &quot;هي متعبة منك..منك فقط..وأنت متعب من كل شيء..&quot;، وقالت لها &quot;هيفاء&quot; صديقتها الكويتية التي تتخاصم دائما وعزيز، بأنه لا يستحقها، وتقول جمانة له اليوم &quot;حبي لك كان أعمى يا عزيز..وأن هيفاء رأت فيك ما لم أره..&quot;، رأت الكذب المتسلسل والخيانة والإنكار الذي يشكك الآخر بنفسه وبعقله وإحساسه، وهي صفات تتجاهلها المرأة التي تحب رجلاً يحبها لكنه غير قادر على الالتزام بها. قال لها بصراحة: &quot;اسمعي.. أنا رجل لعوب.. أشرب وأعربد وأعاشر النساء.. لكنني أعود إليك في كل مرة..&quot;، وكانت تتحمل الأذى: السّفر لأوّل مرّة، تكلّمت أمّها فاطمة بشيء من الفلسفة. حدّثتها عن نبات الياسمين الذي أعطتها اسمه. مثل الياسمين، ربّتها على القناعة والاكتفاء بالقليل. فهو نبات لا يحتاج إلى الكثير من العناية. تكفيه دفعة واحدة من السّماد في ربيع كلّ عام، وتربة رطبة دون فيض من السقيا.\nجميع أنواع الياسمين تفضل النّموّ في مكان مشمس، لكنّها تتحمّل وجود شيء من الظلّ. وشمس تونس كانت مواتية لنضجها وتكوين شخصيّتها، وقد أصبحت جاهزة لتحمّل شيء من ظلالأوروبا ذات المناخ البارد. مثل الياسمين الأبيض المتوسّطي، كانت رقيقة في مظهرها، لكنّ شخصيّتها قويّة وثابتة، مثل رائحة الياسمين النفّاذة والفريدة التي تبثّ إحساسا بالدّفء لا تملكه الورود الأخرى.\nلم تتكلّم عن دلالة الياسمين العاطفيّة التي بحثت ياسمين عنها مذ اهتمّت بمدلولات الزهور في بداية مراهقتها. عرفت أنّ إهداء زهر الياسمين لامرأة يعني “لماذا لا تحبّين أبدًا؟”. والدها أهداها هي، ياسمين، إلى والدتها. كانت آخر عطاياه لها حين تخلّى عن حضانتها إثر الطّلاق. وهي “لم تحبّ بعده أبدًا”. كانت جديرة بتقبّل زهرة الياسمين.\nتفتقد أمها كل يوم أكثر من اليوم الماضي. مع مرور الوقت تزداد يقينًا من ضياعها بدونها. كانت تعلم أن الغربة ليست تجربة سهلة، ومع ذلك وافقت على سفرها. علّمتها كيف تكون ياسمينة حقيقيّة. لكن لعلّها غفلت عن حقيقة مرّة. زهرة الياسمين تذبل بسرعة حين تغادر تربتها وتنسّق في شكل “مشموم” جميل.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"14989178110447422072\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div <span id="freeTextContainer4073522238922318577">كاتب وروائي كويتي<br/> <br/>صدر له عن الدار العربية للعلوم:<br/><br/>- سجين المرايا، رواية 2010<br/>- ساق البامبو، رواية 2012<br/>- فئران أمي حِصَّة، رواية 2015<br/>- حمام الدَّار: أحجية ابن أزرق، رواية 2017<br/>- ناقةُ صالحة، رواية قصيرة 2019<br/><br/>حائز على جائزة الروائية ليلى العثمان لإبداع الشباب في القصة والرواية في دورتها الرابعة وذلك عن رواية سجين المرايا 2010<br/><br/>حائز على المركز الأول في مسابقة "قصص على الهواء" التي تنظمها مجلة "العربي" بالت</span>
  <span id="freeText4073522238922318577" style="display:none">كاتب وروائي كويتي<br /> <br />صدر له عن الدار العربية للعلوم:<br /><br />- سجين المرايا، رواية 2010<br />- ساق البامبو، رواية 2012<br />- فئران أمي حِصَّة، رواية 2015<br />- حمام الدَّار: أحجية ابن أزرق، رواية 2017<br />- ناقةُ صالحة، رواية قصيرة 2019<br /><br />حائز على جائزة الروائية ليلى العثمان لإبداع الشباب في القصة والرواية في دورتها الرابعة وذلك عن رواية سجين المرايا 2010<br /><br />حائز على المركز الأول في مسابقة "قصص على الهواء" التي تنظمها مجلة "العربي" بالتعاون مع إذاعة بي بي سي العربية، عن قصة "البونساي والرجل العجوز"، وذلك في يوليو 2011<br /><br />حصد جائزة الدولة التشجيعية في مجال الآداب وذلك عن رواية "ساق البامبو" عام 2012<br /><br />حصدت روايته "ساق البامبو" الجائزة العالمية للرواية العربية "بوكر" عام 2013<br /><br />حاز لقب شخصية العام الثقافية - جائزة محمد البنكي، في مملكة البحرين 2016<br /><br />رواية "فئران أمي حصة" القائمة الطويلة لجائزة الشيخ زايد 2016-2017<br /><br />رواية "حمام الدار"، القائمة القصيرة لجائزة الشيخ 2018-2019</span>
